================================================================================
# Module 5: Life Cycle Impact Assessment
================================================================================


## 01 Introduction-To-Lcia

Welcome back. Today we're going to have an introduction
to life cycle impact assessment. Our learning objectives for today
are going to be to connect the life cycle inventory to its impacts by defining this
life cycle impact assessment phase of LCA. And in doing so we're going to think about the complex challenges in
addressing some of these impacts. So in LCA, we work hard to get a detailed
list of the emissions and resources. And then we want to think about, how do we connect those to the impacts
on human and environmental health? So in terms of our sustainable development
goal categories, where we have for example responsible consumption and production or
sustainable cities and communities. We want to figure out how does that
impact these other sustainable development goal categories including
good health and well being. Clean water and sanitation,
climate action, life below water and life on land. This is the third and
last phase of a life cycle assessment. So we started with a goal and scope
definition, defining functional units system boundaries,
figuring out the things we're comparing. Then we moved on to
the inventory of extractions and emissions associated with each scenario. What's the energy used? What's the pollution that's
coming into our air and water? And now we move to that last step,
what are the impacts of that inventory? And of course throughout a key
point is interpretation. The four categories of environmental
damage or impact that we focus on here are generally global impacts
that affect the entire world. Human health impacts which
affect humans in general. Ecosystem and biodiversity impact which
affect land, water, soil and species. And then resource use, which is thinking
about the water and land that's used and other resources, minerals as well. So one way to understand these is to
categorize some common environmental health impacts into these categories. So when we're talking about air pollution, we're usually talking about
human health impact categories. When we're thinking about climate change,
that's a global impact that can lead to increased temperatures and
extreme weather around the world. When we're looking at
species becoming extinct, that would fall in
the biodiversity category. When we talk about polluted water
that's related to resource use, but it also is connected to the human health
category of drinking water and food and of course to ecosystem health. And when we look at things like ocean
acidification and sea level rise that can result from climate change, those are also
impacts that happen around the world. And finally whenever we're focusing
on things like use of freshwater, that's a resource use category
that we're focusing on. One thing in life cycle impact assessment
is to think about the balance of the impacts that helps us think about the
balance between using the environment for what humans need versus
misusing the environment. And one example of this would be in
the US Dust Bowl in the 1930s and what happened there was after a period in
the twenties of relatively good rainfall. A lot of farmers in the Great Plains
decided to farm more intensively use deep
plowing techniques and new New developments in machinery
to farm relatively intensively. And so what that did is remove
the native grasses that normally trapped the soil during times
of drought and high winds. And so sure enough in 1930, when a drought
did come, the soil was no longer anchored. And that honor unanchored
soil turned into dust and then the high winds that came blew,
that dust high into the air and blackened the sky and
reached as far as New York City. This was happening throughout
the 1930s and unfortunately, it only intensified the economic impact
of the Great Depression that was already hitting the United States. And so it ended up leaving more
than 500,000 Americans homeless and many who died of dust pneumonia or
malnutrition. And so with things like life
cycle impact assessment, we want to think about the potential
impacts of what we're doing to our environment and how it might impact
people further on and all around. So, in considering impacts, we're facing new challenges
since the dust wall happened. New challenges in the magnitude of
these impacts because of technology and economic development,
we've accelerated and amplified the magnitude of
our footprint and impacts. And this is happening in multiple ways. It's happening in our spatial scale
where globalization distances impacts from the consumer,
from where those impacts occur. So when I consume something, I may not see any direct feedback
of the impact it's having. This also is challenging because with
globalization we require a global response and that makes governance
a challenge when trying to limit anything on a global scale. Another new challenge we're facing
is with the temporal scale. So our current actions may only have
visible impacts decades from now and so that makes it very hard to help
populations limit their actions based on future impacts that they
can't yet see or don't know. And this is all related to more
complex cause-effect chains. These are not always visible and so it's
crucial to use science and technology for diagnostics and to prevent irreversible
impacts that may happen far away in both time and space but
that can still critically impact others. Because as a reminder from before
we do not inherit the earth from our ancestors,
we borrow it from our children. So a summary of this video is the life
cycle impact assessment phase of LCA. Is how we connect that life cycle
inventory of the scenarios we're comparing to the ultimate impacts on
environmental and human health. That's the ultimate
question we want to answer. And because of global interconnectedness
and accelerating development of technology, it's increasingly
challenging to recognize and allocate the impacts of individual and
collective actions


## 02 Overview-Of-Environmental-Impact-Categories-Part-A

Hi, everybody. In today's video, we're going to have an
overview of some of the most common environmental
impact categories for our life cycle
impact assessment. I have to say this
is one of the things that really got me into LCA is realizing that I could
connect the decisions I make with the impacts on humans, on the environment, on animals, in general, be able
to compare those two. Our learning objectives
for today are to first list the four main environmental
damage categories, then we're going to relate specific environmental
and health impacts to each of these categories, and then for a few, we're going to dive deeper
into the mechanisms and the spatial and temporal ranges of some of the dominant impacts. As a reminder, in our phases
of life cycle assessment, we've already talked
about the goal and scope definition
where we did functional units and our different scenarios
and system boundaries. Then we use the inventory of
extractions and emissions to compare the two scenarios and what extractions and emissions
are associated with each. Then in order to make
that information meaningful and useful, we take it the next
step to look at the impacts of those
extractions and emissions. Throughout, of
course, we are doing interpretation to make sure that what we're doing
is making sense, is consistent, and we don't need to revise
anything along the way. The four categories of
environmental damage, we've got global impacts, things like climate change, human health impacts, so these are things that cause death or disease to people. Ecosystem and
biodiversity impacts, so things that affect endangered
species, for example, or the habitats that they
live in, and resource use, things like water use, land use, use of minerals
or non-renewable energy. We'll start with global impacts and we'll look at this through a concept defined by scientists
as planetary boundaries. Now, planetary boundaries, what they do is
they characterize safe operating
space for humanity. Scientists define these
as things where if you transgress one or more
planetary boundary, this may be catastrophic
by triggering nonlinear responses of the continental or
planetary scale. In 2015, in a science
advances paper, scientists characterize
the state of seven out of the nine
planetary boundaries. We'll start by focusing
on climate change, which is right here at the top. We can already see, it's already out of the green safe boundary and into the zone of uncertainty
or increasing risk. The mechanism for climate change is the greenhouse effect. What happens here
is that the sun shines down on the Earth and so sends energy, and light, and heat into the Earth, and then the Earth's surface
absorbs that and re-emits that back as long wave
or heat radiation. It's a different
form of radiation, which means that
greenhouse gases in the atmosphere absorb
that and then they, in turn, re-emit it. Some of it goes out
into space and some of it goes back to the
Earth's surface. This is generally
useful for us and lets us be alive because those greenhouse gases
keep the surface of the Earth warm enough for us
to function, which we like. But as we can see
in recent time, there have been much higher
anthropogenic CO_2 emissions and so that is leading to a warming of the Earth's
atmosphere that is much higher than many of us can
adapt to in many ways. Impacts to date first on
just the temperature, we can look at the
global warming relative to a time
period before we had omitted so much CO_2 and other greenhouse
gases into the atmosphere. Compared to that time
period of 1850-1900, we can see that, at first, there were just the
normal oscillations, right around the 1960s, there were just the normal
temperature oscillations, but then we can see a
clear trend upward. Those oscillations continued but the average moved upward. Something to notice here
is that the model or estimated anthropogenic
warming is tracking well with the observed monthly global
mean surface temperature, the observed averages
are matching modeled averages based on climate change in the
mechanism that I explained. In terms of long-term impacts
on this temporal scale. These could be variable, so it depends how
humans respond, and has large uncertainties due to the complex system
of the atmosphere. If there's no reduction, we're likely to exceed this
threshold right here of 1.5 degrees of warming and
could even reach two degrees. But with faster CO_2
reductions were likely to stay below 1.5 degrees
of warming and we can see that we might
bring us back down. What are the causes
of climate change? Well, like I said, we believe it's
greenhouse gases, and the reason that
scientists have confidence in that is because the observed warming matches the combined
anthropogenic forcing. Forcing is another word for the effect of our emissions on the temperature
that's observed. The observed warming
globally we can see is a little bit more
than half a degree Celsius, that's this black bar, and greenhouse gases, actually, through our models are
predicted to lead to a greater increase in
temperature than is observed. But the reason for
that is that we also, as humans, emit other particles and pollutants and emissions
into the atmosphere, and so those forcings actually end up cooling
the atmosphere, and when we add the green
bar and the yellow bar, we get our combined
anthropogenic forcings, which is matching our
observed warming. Something else to
note here is that the natural forcings due
to solar oscillations or any other aspects of
our orbit are much smaller compared to the observed warming and
the predicted warming. In terms of thinking
of the climate change impacts so far, it can be hard to attribute any given impact of climate change because of
the probabilities involved. But in recent decades, scientists have been
publishing studies that directly can link
climate change to specific impacts and they've
connected climate change to impacts on every single
continent in the world. That's what this is showing us. These impacts are happening
all over the world and they're connecting
all types of impacts. In blue, we've got impacts
on physical systems, such as glaciers or
coastal erosion. In green, we've got our
biological systems, so things like wildfire
and marine ecosystems. Then in red, we've got
human and managed systems, so things like food production or livestock or
health and economies. You can see blue, green,
and red throughout. If you want to dig
deeper into this, you can also see the confidence
range for all of these. You can see relatively
high confidence that climate change is impacting every continent and
the many systems that we use in living. What about future risks? These depend on the future of our greenhouse gas emissions. I'm going to go
through this figure backwards a little bit, starting at Panel C. At Panel C, we can see that our
baseline emissions are in gray at the top-right, that's how much
were predicted to emit in greenhouse gases
if there's no change. But there are
different scenarios that are in the IPCC report, all of which would lead
to varying levels of reduced greenhouse
gas emissions, and so what the
rest of this figure does is look at those different scenarios
and their impacts. In Panel B, what we
can see is that if we are able to reduce our
greenhouse gas emissions, then that would reduce the cumulative
emissions over time. It would go from around 8,000 gigatons of CO_2 down to 6,000, 4,000, or 3,000
gigatons of CO_2. Then on the vertical
axis of Panel B, we can see what that
would mean in terms of the global mean
temperature change. If we make no change, 8,000 would correspond to 4-5
degrees Celsius increase. But as we reduce our
cumulative CO_2 emissions, that would reduce the global
mean temperature change. Then finally, we can
link this to Panel A, the risks from climate change because these are dependent
on the temperature change. What we can see is
that for things like unique and threatened ecosystems or for extreme weather events, even at 1.5-2 degrees Celsius, which we were already
pretty much at, we're experiencing very high
risk due to climate change. But for things like the
distribution of impacts, the global aggregate impacts, or large-scale singular events, if we can keep our
temperature change down, then we can reduce
the risk level, the additional risk for
any of those things.


## 03 Overview-Of-Environmental-Impact-Categories-Part-B

How do we treat this? How do we treat this
issue of climate change? The main thing is to reduce
greenhouse gas emissions. What we're seeing here is that in the lighter
version of each color, we would have the
direct CO_2 emissions for different sectors. If there were no mitigation. We can see for transport, there would be a steadily
increasing CO_2 emissions, for buildings as well. Definitely for industry
and electricity, we also see this trend in increasing CO_2 emissions
associated with those. Now AFOLU here stands for the
net effect of agriculture, forestry, and other land use. Then there's also non CO_2 greenhouse gas
emissions that are coming. But if we have various
mitigation scenarios, those are the darker
version of each color, we can see how we can
reduce the CO_2 emissions. One of the main ones we can
do is for our electricity. Key measures to achieve such mitigation goals
include decarbonizing, so reducing the carbon intensity of electricity generation, as well as efficiency
enhancements and behavioral changes. In order to reduce
our energy demand compared to our baseline. Another thing to think about
in terms of the treatment of this climate change issue is addressing CO_2
emissions fairly. Not just lumping by country and asking different
countries to meet targets, but recognizing our per
capita contributions. In this figure, we can see that the United States and China have similar size areas
on this figure. Which means that they are both contributing similar
size CO_2 emissions. But because the horizontal axis is their annual emissions, and the vertical axis is
the per capita emissions. We can see that, the
US is emitting per person double the CO_2
compared to China. We want to be able to
take that into account when making global plans
about CO_2 emissions. That's one example of global
impact climate change, and it's one we're
struggling with. I also want to focus on a different global impact
or ozone depletion. We can see in our planetary
boundaries figure, that this is one where
we are safely still in the safe or green zone for our stratospheric
ozone depletion. We'll look into
why that might be. The diagnosis about
ozone depletion is that in the 1970s and '80s, a lot of things called CFCs or chlorofluorocarbons
were being emitted as part of industrial processes. What would happen is that the chlorine would get removed, once it's in the atmosphere,
get removed from the CFC, and it was then able to
break down the ozone high in our atmosphere by reacting
with the O_3 ozone molecules, separating off an O. Then the chlorine
could get free and do it again and again and again. Even a small amount of
chlorine could lead to a huge depletion in ozone
in our stratosphere. One CFC could break down
100,000 molecules of ozone. The problem with
this is that the sun has different types of rays
that can do damage to us, and the ozone layer
was blocking some of these critical UV rays from impacting human
and plant health. We were able to measure
this happening. This graph is showing the
minimum levels of ozone over Antarctica
through the year 2015. The lower the
minimum levels are, the more exposed plants and animals are to the
harmful UV radiation. Now we can see a clear
drastic decrease in the minimum ozone, which is bad, but then
they flattened out. The ozone was no longer
decreasing, and so we went up. How did that happen? Here's a little timeline. The key thing is the diagnosis
came before the symptoms. In 1920s, a scientist
named Dobson was studying stratospheric
ozone, and in 1974, an article in nature already
characterized this mechanism of CFCs migrating into the stratosphere and
releasing chlorine. In 1985, the British
Antarctic Survey team had been measuring the ozone
layer already for 18 years, and they saw the first
visible evidence that the ozone layer
was depleting. It was depleted so much they
actually didn't believe their data until a second
set of data confirmed it. Then relatively quickly in 1987, the Montreal Protocol
on Substances That Deplete the Ozone
Layer is signed. It became a legally
binding protocol between 24 countries to
reduce CFC emissions. Kofi Annan, the former Secretary General of
the United Nations, said about this, "Perhaps the single most successful
international agreement to date has been the
Montreal Protocol." That that led to a freeze in the production
and consumption of CFCs, and it was a success story. Industrialized countries in
1986 had a huge amount of ozone depleting
potential each year that they were emitting, and that steadily reduced to a point where we're no longer worried about
the ozone layer. That was a little bit about
the global impacts category. Now we're going to talk about
some human health impacts. One of the dominant
human health impacts is ambient air pollution. We can see that on this
figure from the Lancet, which is outlining the
attributable dailies per year, so disability adjusted
life years per year for different things that
impact human health. The biggest one is dietary
risks and inactivity, which we spent a
lot of time talking about already related
to food and diet. But the next two
biggest ones are household air pollution and ambient particulate matter,
outdoor air pollution. Those have a huge
impact on human health. Other things that impacted
our human toxicity, including indoor
toxicity of chemicals, photochemical ozone. We'll talk a little bit more
about ionizing radiation, noise, occupational
risks and injuries. One of those dominant things is the human health impacts due to primary and secondary
particulate matter, and this is something
that I focused on a lot in my research. It leads to 3.2 million
deaths per year, 65% of which actually
occur in Asia, which is not at all necessary, the country that's
inducing the PM emissions. What happens is something called reactive oxidants stress, which leads to cardiovascular
and pulmonary diseases, lung cancer, lower respiratory
infections, and stroke. The US alone is responsible
for 86,000 deaths per year, 85% of which are
cardiovascular diseases. Another human health impact other than particulate matter is that due to photochemical
oxidation from NOx and VOCs. NOx is formed when nitrogen
and oxygen that are already in the air react together under
high temperatures, such as in the exhaust of
fossil fuel-burning engines, and that creates NOx. NOx can then react with volatile organic
compounds or VOCs, which can be emitted
by gasoline as an antigen pyrogenic source or biogenic sources like trees. When sunlight plus NOx plus
VOCs react, they form ozone. But in this case, the ozone is close to
where we are rather than far up above blocking the sun and it actually has
negative effects on us, on respiratory
effects for humans or decreased reduction
in plant productivity. Those were some examples of human health impacts
associated with emissions. Now we'll talk a
little bit about ecosystem and
biodiversity impacts. The main example
will give here is the Lake Erie toxic algae bloom. This happens when rainfall washes fertilizer
and manure that we emit into the environment from these large farm fields into streams that all
flow into Lake Erie. This fertilizer
causes huge amounts of algae to grow each year, which can make water
toxic to fish, wildlife, and people. As an example, in July 2019, a severe bloom of
blue-green algae began spreading across the
western half of Lake Erie. The dominant organism in
there was a bacteria that produces a toxin which
can cause liver damage, numbness, dizziness,
and vomiting. That's an example of how
our emissions can cause ecosystem impacts that can
also impact human health. Now we'll cover the last
category of resource use. This can include water use, such as freshwater
biodiversity loss, or global water crisis that leads to food
security issues. Land use, which
can be related to habitat change as a major driver of terrestrial
biodiversity loss. Biotic depletion, such as overfishing
and marine ecosystems. Or energy resources, that's a functional impact
where the renewal rate of an energy source
is vastly exceeded and that's why we call
it non-renewable energy. Here's an example
of how water use can affect humans
and ecosystems. Water use decreases
the availability and leads to
competition for water. For human users that don't
have any adaptation, there are direct
human health impacts associated with
lesser, dirtier water. But many adaptations also
decrease water availability to have indirect impacts on human health resources
and ecosystem. For ecosystems, they
get limited water. There are, of course, impacts
on ecosystem quality, both direct and indirect. The summary of all this is that the main diagnoses of these global impact
categories that we're talking about are the
increasing consumption in developed nations is leading to pollution and usage of
non-renewable resources. The negative impacts that are occurring are unequally
affecting off in developing nations through irreversible use of
natural resources, decreasing water quality, or climate change or
particulate matter. It's good to realize
that the scale of these impacts can
range from very local to global and from immediate impacts to those
that take decades to manifest. A key thing here is just to think globally and act locally, and use LCA to help make our actions more
effective. Thank you.


## 04 Principles-And-Framework-Part-A

Welcome back, today we're going to dive
deeper into the framework of life cycle impact assessment, or LCIA. So our learning objectives for today, are to describe the challenges that we
have with combining different indicators. And being able to compare them,
to find the key vocabulary for life cycle impact assessment. Especially focusing on this idea
of midpoint impact categories, and endpoint damage categories. And really recognize that the uncertainty
of impacts depends on interpretation uncertainty and model uncertainty. Which are often inversely related. So, let's say that we want to calculate
the impact of University of Michigan by combining some key numbers. Well it was founded in 1817, and
has more than 64,000 students, and the stadium capacity
is 106,000 students. Wow, these are all really big numbers so
let's get the total impact by just adding up all three and
we get a whopping 172,000. But of course in calculating this,
this doesn't make any sense, those numbers shouldn't be added,
even if all of them are big. We can't compare U of M to UCLA,
by looking at this number 170,000 and seeing which one's better or
more impactful. So but what happens is actually
in calculating impacts for impact assessment of different decisions. Many approaches end up looking for a
single final number to compare two things. And they end up adding things that
don't necessarily make sense. So in impact assessment, our goal is to convert things as
much to common units as possible. And add up those units, so
that they can be added and then compared to other scenarios. Now you've actually already seen
an example of different impact assessment approaches. When Professor Julier presented
the comparison of packaging materials. Where different approaches caused
different emissions to be weighted differently. He was showing you different
impact assessment methods. And what we can see in these
results is that even there it looks like different emissions
we get different results. We don't get the same results for
different impact assessment methods. And so even with a more formal
impact assessment method, different results can happen and
that can reveal a potential for bias. And this is why sometimes when such
impacts are calculated, folks might say, well show me your results, and
I'll just tell you who paid you. I'll tell you who sponsored this work, in order to get results
that make them look better. That's understandable that people are
suspicious of those results because they think it could be skewed
to support the sponsors. So to be more scientific, and
to yield more robust results, we need more recognized and
consistent impact assessment methods. Especially it turns out for toxicity
assessment, without a formal assessment, we all end up adding implicit and
unscientific weightings. So we want to be explicit with
the assumptions we're making. So let's say that we have the question of
how do we compare elephants and apples? Again it depends on your functional unit, like what's the thing you're
comparing them based on. If it's based on having to transport
them and impacts from transport, you care about their weight of course. But if you cared about the caloric intake which I don't like the idea
of eating an elephant at all. But then you would care more about the
impacts associated with getting a certain amount of calories out of it. And so, that's similar to
what we are trying to do here it is a difficult challenge and so
we'll dive into the details of it. Now in recent decades, international progress has been
made on LCIA, method quality. The first approaches did more basic
things like, group emissions and add emissions just based on
where they were emitted to. So, if I have one chemical
emitted to water and another chemical emitted to water. I can just group those because they're
both admitted to water independent of the ultimate impact they have,
that's called critical volumes. And there was another method
that what it did is it looked at environmental policy would define,
a critical flow of emissions. Like we're having a lot of this
pollutant emitted into the air or water, which isn't good. And then for a given scenario would
see how much extra was being emitted associated with that scenario. Is it close already to its critical flow? And so
the closer it is to its critical flow, the more impact it was considered
to have that's called ecopoints. But neither of these methods linked
to the ultimate fate exposure and impact of the actual emission. And so it turns out Leiden University in
the Netherlands played a significant role, in structuring the field in
terms of impact categories. Looking at fate exposure and impact and, I was lucky enough randomly to be
going to school at Leiden University. And meet some of the people
including Professor Hye Yoons, who helped come up with these methods. So one of the key aspects of LCIA is to
start with what we're trying to protect. We want to evaluate damage
to the things that we value. So things we want to protect our the human
system, the natural environment and of course life support
systems like our climate. And there are two different types of
values that can be considered here. There's the intrinsic value of we just
don't want people to get sick or die for human health. And we don't want species to go extinct,
for ecosystem quality. And there's also the instrumental value,
so the socioeconomic value. So do impacts, decrease the value
of our built infrastructure, or decrease the value of cash crops. Or in terms of natural resources, do our impacts decrease the amount
of minimal resources available. Or, the services that can be
provided by those ecosystems. Now of course there's also cultural value, such as valuing the cultural heritage
of historic buildings or monuments. Or value of the natural heritage or
flora, fauna and geology. And at the moment LCIA hasn't
really delved into that realm, but I hope that it does soon. And so the way that we look at
the damage to these areas of protection, is described somewhat in ISO 14044. Which describes an impact
category that we can use an LCIA. Which means we take our life
cycle inventory results, and we assign these results to
different impact categories. And we use impact modeling to compare the
midpoint damage, at different categories. We can then use damage modeling or
qualitative approaches, to compare this midpoint damage. To ultimately a damage to one
of our areas of protection. That's the overview, so
let's dive a little bit deeper into that. The UNEP-SETAC framework, describes
this midpoint and damage, framework. So we have our emissions to air water or
soil and resource extractions, and these can each be linked to
different midpoint impact categories. So for example emission of CFCs,
can be linked both to ozone depletion and to climate change because
it's a greenhouse gas. These midpoint impacts can then be
grouped together to look at their damage, their endpoint damage. For example to human health,
ecosystem quality, natural resources. And again, a given midpoint impact it's
okay for it to be added up together, to connect to different damage categories. So ozone depletion and climate
change can affect human health, but climate change can also
affect ecosystem quality. Now in this framework, it is allowed to then combine the damage
categories into a single weighted score. Because people often want to
compare an array of scenarios. And they just want to get a final
score for each scenario and see which one looks best. But that does get a little
bit like are UFM example, where you're then adding things
that don't have similar units. And have to apply your own weighting and
your own biases of what you value most. And so, we usually wouldn't take it to
the next level of a weighted score.


## 05 Principles-And-Framework-Part-B

Let's understand this a little bit
better by applying it to climate change. So in the LCI results we calculate
the amount of greenhouse gas emissions associated with different scenarios and this is assigned to the climate
change impact category. We calculate this midpoint damage
by using the radiative forcing that each emission causes to the atmosphere. How much is it warming the atmosphere? And then add this up because it's
all now in the same ultimate impact. And we can then convert this if we want
to end point damages to human health and ecosystem quality by indicating
how it can lead to wildfires, flooding, heat stress etcetera. And let me break that down a little bit
more into a cause effect flow diagram for climate change. We have the emissions into the atmosphere, we have the time integrated concentration
of having those emissions stick around in the atmosphere and
absorb that extra radiation. This leads to a radiative forcing
a warming of the atmosphere which causes climate change, increased temperature,
extreme weather, precipitation, droughts. And that can have effects on ecosystems
such as affecting their net primary production or effects on humans
such as flooding or malnutrition. And so, we can see that
the climate change part and rated enforcing is our midpoint level. And then the ultimate damage
to effects on humans and ecosystems is our end point level. I put this slide in here because I know
I'm throwing around a lot of terms and so we wanted to collect them all
on the slide for your reference. We have an example of LCI results or
our greenhouse gas emissions. Example of our midpoint impact
category as climate change, example of our category indicator
is our global warming potential or kilograms equivalent CO2
per kilogram emitted. The characterization model used for this example is IPCC model that
connects emissions to impacts. Our result indicator is our
kilograms equivalent CO2. We bring everything back to that. And our end point damaged categories that
we've talked about here are human health like heat stress and ecosystem qualities
such as coral impacts on coral reefs. To understand the types of modeling
that can go into this impact assessment, let's better understand this category
indicator of a global warming potential. So Global Warming Potential of
a substance i measures the increase in radiative forcing of the atmosphere
due to the emission i. Or basically,
how much radiation is it absorbing and causing the atmosphere to warm up by? And this is all measured relative
to what a kilogram of CO2 would be? So, our climate change midpoint impact
score, S here, of course depends on the mass of substance i admitted and
its Global Warming Potential. How much is it increasing
the warming of the atmosphere? And the total score is the sum of all
the different substances emitted, their mass and
their Global Warming Potential. Now, what's helpful to understand here
is this isn't necessarily simple. The Global Warming Potential has
another complicated equation with integrations that involves
the concentration of our substance i, the absorption of thermal
radiation of that molecule. The time horizon considered because
different substances have different lifetimes in the atmosphere. And let me dive just a little bit deeper
into that as you can follow me in so that we can see how the choices that
need to be made when we're comparing different things. So the magnitude of climate change impacts
depends on her time horizon considered. And the reason is that each molecule has
a different lifetime in the atmosphere. CO2 hurricanes around for 300 to 1000
years, so it's considered indefinite here. And it's short and long term impacts
by definition are 1kg equivalent CO2. But if we want to compare this to a
different scenario that results in methane emissions, methane lifetime is only
12 years in the atmosphere, but it has a higher reading
enforcing than CO2. So shorter term, it has a higher global
warming potential than CO2, 34 is its GWP. But if we wanted to look longer term
since the methane decays faster over longer timescales, it's not quite as
much greater than carbon dioxide, it only has a factor of 11. Another example to help us understand
a little better is nitrous oxide. Its lifetime is 120 years,
so a bit closer to CO2. Which means that it's shorter term impacts
of 298 aren't that much different from its longer term impacts of 297 because it
lives relatively long in the atmosphere. And I hope this helps illustrate that even
with all of our equations and modeling, there's definitely uncertainty in
these models and choices that we make that we need to make explicit so
that the results can be trusted. Now, to qualitatively understand this
uncertainty at various midpoint and end points parts in our interpretation,
we can see this figure. And this figure tells us that when
we just look at the LCI inventory, we know the emissions and
extractions relatively well. So our model uncertainty is low,
but we don't know what they mean. I can't compare a kilogram admitted of
one chemical to a kilogram admitted of another chemical without knowing
the impact that they have. So our interpretation uncertainty is
relatively high at LCI inventory stage. And we try to reduce that interpretation
uncertainty better understand the impact by connecting these
to midpoint impact categories. But in doing that, we increase our model
uncertainty because we have to add these equations and models to estimate
what impact the emissions will have. And even at the midpoint level,
we haven't yet connected it to these big categories
of human and environmental health. So if we want to go further to our
endpoint damage, that leads to greater ability to interpret, so
lower interpretation uncertainty. If I just say, this one has more human
health damage than this other one, you know a little bit
more what I mean by that. But it has greater model uncertainty and
that it depends on the time horizons considered and
it depends on how we waited things and how we combine different midpoint
impacts into our endpoint damage. So as we increase our model uncertainty,
we are increasing our relevance that leads to a balance
that LCA folks need to make. Where in the LCI inventory, we have
relatively high overall uncertainty. At the endpoint damage, we have relatively
high uncertainty due to our modeling. And so this midpoint damage is often
what a lot of LCA folks like to focus on because it's where we are minimizing
our overall uncertainty, by balancing out our model uncertainty and
our interpretation uncertainty. So to summarize what we talked about
today is how to take our LCI framework, LCIA framework and how that connects
our life cycle inventory results of emissions to air water,
soil and resource extractions. Can connect these to their
midpoint damage categories, which we've listed here including ozone
depletion, ecotoxicity, eutrophication in order to better understand
the impacts on humans and environments. And then if we want,
we can add these up and sum them up in different ways to
ultimately lead to end point damage or to human health, ecosystem quality,
natural resources, etcetera. These things that we ultimately
want to protect or value. But as we move over to the right,
we are increasing our model uncertainty. And so, we need to balance this balance
of making the results more relevant, but also knowing they're
becoming more uncertain. Thank you very much.

at midpoint level to our car case study. And we want to identify

at midpoint level. So within each category we can look and

## 01 Interpretation-Principles

Interpretation, the
force phase of LCA. What are the basic principles
for interpretation? It's looking at your
product results from all the different points
of view that you can do, from an angle, from
another angle, and this will build your
conclusion of your study. We're really focusing on
the interpretation phase, the last phase of my life cycle. We know that interpretation
has already taken place as we were analyzing
the goal definition, as we analyze the inventory. But it's really at the end of the impact

## 01 Checks-And-Uncertainty-Part-A

We have so many number in LCA. Millions of number. How can we ensure

associated with them? So learning objective is to perform

of an uncertainty assessment. Be able to estimate

## 02 Checks-And-Uncertainty-Part-B

between scenario. This is for part B. First, considering the

the intermediary flows. An emission factor that we

in the model. Second model type, the

murderer uncertainty. You could miss some
contribution here. We're trying also to
account for that. Some choices are associated
with uncertainties. For example, the choice of
the allocation methods, the choice of your
system boundaries, and the time horizons that you consider will also did
a 100 years larger than a 100 years that will play a role, and the choice
of your weighting methods. Now, variable, it's either spacial viability

account for that, then it's just
viability, and you are able to reproduce that but

can change with time. New technology can

choices is well-defined. How you should define

significantly different. Flows like energy and CO_2, you need at least a 10
percent difference. There are flows where
data are relatively good, so small difference
is important. But if you have a two
percent difference, you can consider them as equal. Acidification, eutrophication,
fine particulate at least 30 to I would say 30-50 percent
difference for the PM. Because uncertainty
on the emission here is more substantial. For toxicity results. Here the uncertainty
is on the inventory, but also on the
characterization factor and the impact side. Here, at least you need at least a factor
of ten difference. Or if it's very different
substances and type of impact, a factor 100 to be
considered as significant. The same for damage
oriented results if you compare across
different impact category, fine particulate
with global warming, with human toxicity,
at least a factor of 10 or a 100 different to have
a significant difference. Question. Is that
relevant still, two ways to use factors and characterization
factor which have so much uncertainty? The answer, you have to realize that toxicity results between saccharine and dioxane has 12 orders of
magnitude variation. Here we are speaking to one
or two orders of magnitude. 10-100 is discriminant compare to a million times a million. It's still useful. But look at these
results in log scale, either for talks here or when
you compare damage results. Sensitivity analysis
and scenario analysis. Sensitivity analysis enabled you to test the robustness of data to the data input
and an assumption. The idea is, you vary the input, you look how the output
vary. Two options. First is you vary each input parameter
by 10 percent and you see if the output vary also by 10 percent, or very little. Problem here you're not
considering that some of these parameters here are
more uncertain than other. Not all parameter are equal. It's better in fact, to vary the input
parameter between the upper and the lower 95
percent confidence limits. You estimate for a
given parameter. This parameter vary
perhaps by 10 percent, but these other parameter
vary by 100 percent, a factor two twice higher, twice lower, for example. You make this
variation according to the given parameter,
and you look at the variation in
final impact estimates. The scenario analysis is a
sensitivity analysis but with discrete changes like change the method of allocation
that you are comparing. For example, instead
of substitution, you use affects
financial allocation. You'd look how this
affects the final results, how the results are
robust for this. Now, sensitivity
analysis usually study one parameter at a time. Now, you can already put sense. This is where you vary different parameter in
fractural experimental plan, for example, where
you take extreme and check all the extreme
from a square. Or then you can perform really
uncertainty propagation. Here you can use the cardinal Monte Carlo
random parameter selection. You do that for the input. You get a results on the output and you repeat
that median of time, and you get a distribution
on your output there. Or they are simpler
way to do that, which is a Taylor series
expansion that I will demonstrate through
an example later on. Finally, empirical
evaluation is really the only way I say
to model validity, but not that easy
to apply for LCA.

the ratio or difference in final score. Let's apply that to our case. First, which type of distribution
are we using in LCA? Very often log normal distribution, why? Because we are limited usually for most of our data at 0,
we are bounded at 0. Why we are there,
because you cannot have negative emission, or you cannot have consumption
of a resource which is negative. So when you have data which is limited on
the lower side but not on the upper side, often you will have a distribution
like a log normal distribution which has a longer tail on the upper part
because here you have no limitation. So the uncertainty is higher on that side. How is a log normal
distribution parameterized? We start from here. The geo means the mean of the log,
which is not the top by the way of the distribution,
it's a bit on the right of the top. And this mean of the log we will
consider what we call the GSD square or the square geometric standard deviation, also called sometime in certain cases,
the coefficient of variation. And this is a multiplicative parameters,
okay? So the 95th confidence interval will
be between an upper and a lower limit. And you see that the upper limit
is bigger than the lower limit. And if I have a GSD square of 2,
this means that the lower limit will be equal to the geo
mean divided by 2 and the upper. So I will have here 280 divided by 2, that's about 140, okay? And on the upper side, I will have in fact around here 500 that will be twice the GSD square, okay? And that would be in fact more than
500 in term of the distribution. So we should have in fact

about 50 percent of chance, it goes one direction
or the other. These are two
comparable scenarios. Let us conclude LCA with

your quality control. I expect you will do that
with your case study. Come to me if you have question, we fix the meeting,

for what we need for LCA. When performing an uncertainty

## 01 Lca-Summary-Lca-Good-Practices-In-One-Page

We have looked at the different
phases of LCA in details, looking at all the data methods,
way to interpret for good practice. It's now time to wrap up everything and
summarize what are the key parameters the cree criteria for
a good practice in LCA. And let's do that in 12 minutes. The learning objective is to identify the
critical points that affect LCA results. Provide principles for good practice for
each of these influential points. Synthesize LCA strengths and limitations. We have been looking through
the lifecycle impact the LCA chapter of our course at
the four phases here of LCA, at the impact of particulate and
chemical in product on human health. And we are now doing
the synthesis before going from product to sustainable consumption, which will constitute our last chapter. It's pretty cool that we can
summarize the key points and the critical points for
good LCA practice in a single slide, okay? And this slide,
you could use it when you do a review or want to use an LCA looking
how the author have been addressing all these critical points. And this gives you a sort of checklist
to look what are the good practice for each of these points. So let's look at them. First, we recommend to perform
an LCA with a screening approach. So carry it in two steps. A first screening phase, okay, which will
take about half of the total time and where you cover the entire LCA but
look at orders of magnitude. And then you choose what
needs to be improved, refined because it's important or
brings substantial uncertainty, and you perform a detailed analysis
of these points which are important. And that's valid for
many scientific approach. Second, for the goal definition,
remember the popcorn, how the functional unit was crucial and
could completely change results. So be sure and check that the functional
unit is really representing the main function and the service of the product
and is a good representation of that. Should be metric which increases
if the functional unit doubles. For example,
the emission should also double. So it's an extensive metric which
is not a ratio, so it's not. Units are not something per
something in that case. System boundaries, third critical point
of the goal and system definition. It should cover the same
functional reality. Remember the fast food example where if
we use geographical or business boundary, this is likely not to represent the same
functional reality in all scenarios. You can exclude negligible processes and
for this you can define a cutoff where
processes can only be excluded if they contribute to less than this
cutoff percentage to the total impact. Processes that are identical
in the different scenario can potentially be excluded, but
only if the intermediary flows of these processes are strictly
equal between scenario. So if the quantity use of this process
per functional unit is exactly the same, and beware, because if the yield,
the outcome is different, the total output is different, it's likely
that this flow will also be different. Allocation between co-products,
like between wheat and stroke. What are the hierarchy recommended by ISO? First, we try to separate the process and
attribute all or some of the processes to one co-product or
the other. Could we separate them? Second, we want to consider
the possibility to do to use system expansion where
we will extend the system boundary to include a proven
substituted product. And then account for the avoided emission
of this product as a bonus to our impacts. Third, we can use physical
allocation based on mass or energy content, but
only if there is a direct causality. So don't use mass if total impact
are not directly related to mass. Finally, we can use
financial allocation nearly in every case as a comparative
allocation method or as a default allocation method when
we cannot apply the other method. And again,
we are based on causality as a measure of the incentive here for production. On the inventory sides of extraction and
emission of resources and emission, data consistency and
quality is crucial. So, beware to take the database
which are consistent, which are comprehensive,
covering all necessary inventory flows, and don't mix easily databases
unless you really need to. And check in that case that for
example electricity mix provide comparable results in
the different databases. Otherwise, you might have
real biases in your results. Third, if we move to the impact
assessment phase of the LCA, you first select LCA methods that cover
the entire cause effect chain, okay? And I would recommend in fact
to go presently to damage and use methods which go to damage by default. We need to include many information
on fate, transport, dilution, degradation, and effect like intrinsic
toxicity, soil species sensitivity. Consider and compare the contribution of
each impact category to total damages. Don't aggregate everything and
you get a final score, and you base all your interpretation
on the final score. Check that the conclusions are robust for
the main impact category which are contributing to more than
a percent to the total impact. And use several impact
assessment methods in parallel to test results robustness like
IMPACT World+ or ReCiPe that we have here. Finally, for interpretation, compare
results from different angles, okay? Look at each LCA phase, the inventory
results, the midpoint characterization results and the damage characterization
of the impact assessment, and the normalization or
weighting results that we have. Look at the contribution
of each life cycle stage, raw material extraction,
product manufacturing, production, use, waste and disposal, and
of each component of your product. And especially focus on the one
generating the largest impact or those where low cost enabled
to have efficient solution. Consider each substance, okay? Each elementary flow and
each impact category contribution. Perform a sensitivity study to
look at the significance and test the significance of
the differences between scenario and the robustness of your main conclusions. LCA, in conclusion, has really
matured tremendously in the last year. And one sign among other is
publication now in Nature on LCA of international trade
which drives biodiversity, which was from a colleague in Australia. And this paper on looking at
the rethinking of the global supply chain which was presenting to
a large republic the basis of LCA. Now to conclude and get an overview, I would recommend you to
have a look at the chapter 8 of our book on
Environmental Life Cycle Assessment, which give you LCA through
an example from A to Z. From treating urban sewage sludge, which is an interesting,
and will enable you to have a sort of recap
of the anti method. In conclusion for this chapter three,
the LCA system approach and the system character of this
approach is a strength. And provide the ability to screen and identify hotspots across
the entire product lifecycle, looking at multiple types of impacts and
analyzing multiple trade-offs. The good news is that data
availability for both inventory and impact assessment has substantially
increased and really facilitates LCA application as you did in relatively
a short time in your case study. As a system approach,
uncertainty remains high and need to be acknowledged and
accounted for when informing decision. Finally, so
far we looked one product at a time. This is useful, but this might not be
sufficient to bring us to sustainable consumption and that's why we have
a fourth chapter in this class.
